HOME      REVIEW OF GHANA LAW 1991 - 92
 

CONVENTIONAL NORMS AND NATIONAL OBLIGATIONS: GHANA AND THE EXCLUSIVE ECONOMIC ZONE [1991-92] VOL. 18 RGL 220—243

SARPONG G A

 

*

INTRODUCTION

One of the most significant developments since Alvid Pardo's proposal before the United Nations General Assembly (UNGA) urging the world community to regard the ocean floor beyond the limits of national jurisdiction as the "common heritage of mankind" is the development and crystallisation of the Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ) as an established institution of international law. The 200-mile zone extending from the baselines of coastal states out into the sea is a multi-purpose and multi-functional zone that caters for a two-fold utilisation of the seas: it allocates resource rights to coastal states while guaranteeing at the same time navigational rights of all other states.

The EEZ concept evolved as a viable compromise between extremist states who would like to exercise wide jurisdictional rights over the seas and moderate states who were bent on keeping the expansion of states into the seas to a minimum so as to enhance navigational interests, amongst other things.

The juridical nature of the EEZ has been a subject of controversy particularly in its formative years. Some states perceive the EEZ as the high seas whilst others see it as the territorial sea. The EEZ is neither; in fact it is a zone sui generis; and although certain jurists see this controversy as now purely of academic interests1 in view of the provisions of the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea,2 an examination of the subject is still worthwhile for a number of reasons: The provisions on the EEZ are scattered throughout the Convention; and in some cases do not lend themselves to easy comprehension and/or interpretation. Besides, there is no uniformity and/or consistency of state practices including legislation on the subject. An analysis of the subject therefore is thus necessary so as to put issues in the right perspective. This is all the more important in view of the fact that the Convention requires only thirteen more ratifications to come into force.3 In any event, even if the Convention does not come into force the EEZ concept is now widely recognised as part of customary international law.4  Furthermore, for states like Ghana that have ratified the Convention, the latter is the norm or benchmark against which the validity or otherwise of their municipal legislation may be assessed. In this regard, an examination of Ghanaian legislation on the EEZ reveals certain shortcomings that ought to be rectified in line with our conventional obligations.

The purpose of this paper is thus two-fold: First, to provide an insight into the EEZ concept as an institution of international law. Secondly, in the light of the analysis, assess existing Ghanaian legislation to ascertain the extent to which our national legislation conform to our conventional obligations. The ensuing discussion, it is hoped, would help throw further light on the EEZ concept and the nature of the various competencies that may be exercised by Ghana in the light of UNCLOS.5

THE ORIGIN AND NATURE OF THE EEZ

The roots of the EEZ concept lies in President Truman of the United States' Proclamation of 1945 (the Truman Proclamation).6 In the Proclamation, the US asserted jurisdiction over the resources of its continental shelf. The Proclamation however made it clear that the US did not intend to affect the character as high seas of the waters above the continental shelf and their right to free and unimpeded navigation. It was this Proclamation, made in the wake of the discovery of hydrocarbons in the US continental shelf then, that initiated the functional approach to jurisdiction into the seas. Indeed, it would be difficult to deny that the claim by the US in respect of the non-living resources if the continental shelf was a claim to an economic zone of exclusive coastal state jurisdiction.7 The proclamation became an attractive precedent especially for the Latin American states who followed suit with sometimes contradictory and even fantastic claims.8 Eventually, by the Santo Domingo Declaration of 1972 the concept of "patrimonial sea" was accepted by the majority of the Latin American states as representing their view on the subject. This gave the states rights to assert jurisdiction up to 200 miles from the baselines; twelve miles of territorial seas and the remainder an external patrimonial sea zone for resource control subject to the exercise of high seas freedoms like navigation and overflight.

Of course this expansion of states' sovereignty into the seas was a source of worry for maritime states like Great Britain who viewed same as an infringement of navigational rights and stuck to a conservative 3-mile limit as representing their view on the subject. There were unsuccessful attempts by the United Nations — the First and Second UN Conferences on the Law of the Sea in 1958 and 1960 respectively — to resolve the issue of the breadth of the territorial sea. It was the Third UN Conference on the Law of the Sea,9 that was to resolve the issue of the breadth of the territorial sea and nurture into fruition the EEZ. What was finally agreed upon in the Convention is an EEZ area starting at the outer edge of the territorial sea and extending up to a limit of 200 miles from the baseline where the territorial sea is measured. From there, coastal states enjoy sovereign rights to the resources of the sea bed, of the subsoil and of the superjacent waters as well as jurisdiction with regard to maritime scientific research, environmental preservation and the establishment of artificial islands, installations and structures. Interwoven with these rights of the coastal states are the rights of all other states to the enjoyment of the traditional high seas freedoms of navigation, overflight and the laying of submarine cables and pipelines.10 The 200 miles right enures to states without the requirement of any proof of any special circumstances. There is however the requirement for a formal declaration of such a zone unlike, for example, the continental shelf whose existence needs not be declared.11

The Common Heritage of Mankind

The initiative for UNCLOS dates from the Maltese Proposal before the United Nations General Assembly (UNGA) urging the world community to regard the area beyond the limits of national jurisdiction as "the common heritage of mankind." Alvid Pardo, the Maltese delegate, proffered the idea to meet certain concerns: to halt the arms race into the seas and also to ensure that the resources of the sea bed were exploited not only by the technologically superior states but for the benefit of mankind as a whole. This proposal was attractive, and received the active support, especially of the developing states who, lacking the technology to exploit the area by themselves, saw in it a means of sharing in the wealth of the oceans. A number of resolutions on the question of the reservation of the sea bed and ocean floor exclusively for peaceful purposes and the use of their resources in the interest of mankind as a whole were adopted. By Resolution 2340 (XXII), the UNGA established an ad hoc committee to study the peaceful uses of the sea bed and the ocean floor beyond the limits of national jurisdiction.

Further, by Resolution 2749, the UNGA adopted the "Declaration of Principles" governing the sea bed and the ocean floor, and the subsoil thereof; and by Resolution 2750 adopted on the same day, decided to convene in 1973 a conference on the law of the sea; and by Resolution 3067 (XXVIII), in recognition of the fact that the problems of the seas were closely interrelated and thus needed to be considered as a whole, the UNGA decided that the mandate of UNCLOS was the adoption of a convention dealing with all matters relating to the law of the sea.

Participation in the conference were not only states (numbering 57 at the close of the conference), but some specialised agencies of the United Nations as well as liberation movements. The work of the conference was conducted in committees, and all decisions in accordance with the "gentleman's agreement", were arrived at by consensus with no voting on such matters until all efforts at consensus had been exhausted. The final act of the adoption of the convention was, however, by voting. A record 119 states signed the Convention at Montego Bay, Jamaica in December 1982.

Ghana, Other African States and UNCLOS

The EEZ concept originated in Africa.12 It was first advocated by Kenya at the 1971 Colombo Session of the Afro-Asian Consultative Committee (AACC).  The concept was indorsed by the Organisation of African Unity (OAU) Council of Ministers in their May 1973 Declaration of the issues of the Law of the Sea.  In July of that year, Kenya together with thirteen other African states submitted draft articles on the EEZ to the UN Sea Bed Committee. The articles form the basis of the EEZ as enunciated under the  Convention.13

ÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿsources are to be exploited under international arrangements under the Convention, these resources in the EEZ are exclusive to the coastal state in the sense that if it does not choose to explore or exploit it, that is its own affair, but no one else may do so without its consent.22

The view that the EEZ is not the high seas is also supported by certain provisions of the Convention: (1) the exclusion of the high seas freedom of fisheries from the EEZ; (2) the definition of the high seas, inter alia, as all parts of the sea not included in the exclusive economic zone . . . of a state; (3) the requirement that states in the EEZ shall have due regard for the rights and duties of the coastal state and shall comply with the laws and regulations adopted by the coastal state23; and (4) article 59 on attribution of rights does not resolve doubts in favour of the high seas. Such doubts are to be resolved on the basis of "equity." In addition to fisheries, the more novel right of scientific research, is also subject to the jurisdiction of coastal states. Perhaps the only important high seas freedom left in the EEZ is freedom of navigation; but even here, coastal state enforcement of vessel source pollution in the name of living resource conservation is a potential source of threat to freedom of navigation in the zone.

The EEZ, then, is not the same as the high seas, for the denudation of the high seas freedoms of other states from the zone as well as the Convention's provisions on the matter debunk any notion of its being the high seas.

The EEZ in its origin was dictated mainly by economic considerations devoid of any pretences to state sovereignty. In this, it differs from the territorial seas whose evolution was motivated by security considerations. Under the 1958 Convention on the Territorial Seas, state sovereignty over the airspace, over the territorial sea as well as to its bed and subsoil was recognised, albeit the Convention failed to establish the breadth of the zone. This was made subject to "innocent passage" couched in terms of its being non-prejudicial to the peace, good order, or security of the coastal state.24

The Convention establishes a 12-mile territorial sea breadth as opposed to the EEZ’s 200-mile limit. It elaborates, in its article 19, a regime of innocent passage by itemising activities that render passage non-innocent, as well as matters over which coastal states may legislate (article 21). With regard to navigation, a significant development is the trend towards international standards of enforcement: pollution must be serious and wilful to render passage non-innocent; international, not national standards only, are permitted.  In this wise, there is a resemblance to pollution control in the EEZ where international standards are the rule.

However, other features of the territorial sea such as rights to submarine cables and pipelines, sovereignty over the airspace, sea bed and subsoil as opposed to functional rights in the EEZ, and control over military activities, distinguish the territorial sea from the EEZ. Further, the coastal state may suspend temporarily in specified areas of its territorial sea the innocent passage of foreign ships if such suspension is essential for the protection of its security, including weapons exercise.25 What constitutes "temporary suspension" as well as the necessary security consideration are within the discretionary competence of coastal states. This rule is inapplicable in the EEZ. Indeed, here, there is a requirement of prompt release of vessels on posting of a bond in the event of violations of international standards.

The EEZ then cannot be assimilated into the territorial sea; neither can it be subsumed under the high seas. It is a zone sui generis: in simple terms, the rights to resources belong to the coastal state and, in so far as such rights are not infringed, all other states enjoy the freedom of navigation.26  The “sui generis" juridicial nature of the EEZ may be looked at from its three elements: the right and duties accorded coastal states by the Convention; the rights and duties of all other states; and the regulations of activities which do not fall within either category.27 The Convention's texts are the authoritative pronouncements on the subject. To these we must now turn.

RIGHTS AND DUTIES OF COASTAL STATES

The provisions dealing with coastal states' rights in the EEZ are scattered throughout the Convention. To ascertain these, one must have recourse to provisions on the EEZ proper, the continental shelf, the high seas, protection and preservation of the marine environment, scientific research and the settlement of disputes. The subject may be looked at under the broad headings, namely rights to resources and environmental preservation.28

Coastal States' Rights to Resources

Coastal states have sovereign rights for the purpose of exploring and exploiting, conserving and managing the non-living resources of the EEZ. These rights are to be exercised under the provisions on the continental shelf; which is the natural prolongation of the coastal state's land territory out into the sea. Under article 77 of the Convention, the resources consists of the mineral and other non-living resources of the seabed and subsoil together with living organisms belonging to sedentary species. The rights are exclusive in the sense that if the coastal state does not explore or exploit same no one may undertake to do so without the express consent of the coastal state.

Article 56 also grants coastal states sovereign rights for the purpose of exploring and exploiting, conservation and management of the living resources of the EEZ. The rights and correlative duties of the coastal state are spelled out in articles 61 to 73 of the Convention. In the EEZ, the right to determine the total allowable fisheries catches is the sole preserve of the coastal state which is also required to adopt measures that would prevent over-exploitation; that it may do in co-operation with relevant competent international organisations. The coastal state also has the competence to determine its capacity to harvest the living resources of the EEZ. It is however enjoined to permit other states access to the surplus of the allowable catch when it does not have the capacity to harvest the entire allowable catch. Nationals of other states fishing in the EEZ are obliged to comply with the regulatory measures imposed by the coastal states. These regulatory measures are as provided under article 62 of the Convention.29

It must be pointed out, however, that access of other states to the surplus of the allowable catch can be gained only through arrangements with the coastal states. The coastal state is enjoined to do so having regard to articles 69 and 70 which grant landlocked and geographically disadvantaged states (LGDS) rights to participate in EEZ surplus exploitation. In granting other states access to its EEZ, the coastal state should be guided by factors like the significance of the living resources of the area to the economy of the coastal state concerned and its other national interests; the requirements of developing states in the sub-region or region and the need to minimise economic dislocation in states whose nationals have habitually fished in the zone or which have made substantial efforts in research and identification of stocks.

It is important to note that the determination of the total allowable catch is the sole competence of the coastal states. Even though the coastal state is enjoined, as noted above, to permit other states to participate in the exploitation of the surplus, the Convention is silent as to what happens in the event that a coastal state refuses other states access to her surpluses in the EEZ. Being no more than agreement to agree, the articles are inherently weak and cannot enure to the benefit of LGDS in the even of a conflict.

This view is reinforced by the Convention's provisions on dispute settlement which exempt fisheries disputes in the EEZ from compulsory jurisdiction: Under article 297(3), disputes concerning the interpretation or application of the Convention with regard to fisheries are to be settled in accordance with section 2 (on compulsory procedures entailing binding decisions) except that the coastal state shall not be obliged to accept the submission to settlement of any dispute relating to its sovereign rights with respect to the living resources in the EEZ or their exercise including its discretionary process for determining the allowable catch, its harvesting capacity, the allocation of surpluses to other states and the terms and conditions established in its conservation and management laws and regulations.30

Arrests and Punishment for Violations

The coastal states’ jurisdiction over fisheries applies to its own nationals and foreigners alike. Any person found violating the fisheries laws and regulations may be arrested by the competent authorities of the coastal state, tried and punished by its domestic courts. However, states do not have unfettered rights and/or discretion as to the mode of exercise of their criminal jurisdiction. Indeed, the Convention imposes limitations as to the nature and/or type of punishment that may be meted out to a vessel and/or its crew who are apprehended for violating a coastal state's fisheries laws. Article 73, the relevant provision on the subject, provides that arrested vessels and their crew shall be promptly released upon the posting of reasonable bond or other security. In addition, penalties for such violations may not include imprisonment, in the absence of agreement to the contrary by the states concerned; or any other form of corporal punishment.

The Convention further obliges coastal states to promptly notify the flag state, through appropriate channels, of the action taken and of any penalties subsequently imposed. In its judicial proceedings, the coastal state is obliged to impose financial penalties only and to refrain from any or unnecessary detention of a vessel or its crew. The Convention does not provide for the amount of financial penalties that may be imposed. The matter is within the competence of the coastal state. The coastal state also has jurisdiction, as noted, over the establishment of artificial islands, installations and structures as well as the conduct of marine scientific research. As in the case of fisheries, a decision by a coastal state to order suspension or cessation of a research project in its EEZ cannot be questioned.31 The Convention also grants coastal states sovereign rights with regard to other activities appertaining to the economic exploration and exploitation of the zone such as the production of energy from the water currents and winds.32 The provision is framed to allow coastal states to take advantage of technological advancements.

Ghanaian Legislation and the EEZ

At independence, Ghana inherited the conservative three-mile territorial sea breadth in line with British practice on the subject.33 Subsequently, by an Act of Parliament,34 Ghana enacted a twelve-mile territorial sea law in 1963. The breadth of the territorial sea was further extended to a limit of 30 nautical miles in 1973.35 In a dramatic move, Ghana in 1977 extended her territorial sea to 200 miles from the ÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿ it falls short in one area—the environmental aspect.

Environmental Protection in the EEZ

Article 56 makes applicable in the EEZ Part XII of the Convention. This is a comprehensive regime for the protection and preservation of the marine environment with emphasis on vessel source pollution control. Environmental matters may not directly be a resource issue but could be so indirectly; pollution of the seas for instance could be harmful to fisheries, hence these provisions covering the EEZ are quite apposite.  The Convention enjoins states to protect and preserve the marine environment; it emphasises that the exploitation of their resources must be in accordance with the duty to protect and preserve the environment.  States are further enjoined to take measures to deal with all sources of pollution from the marine environment; from land based sources; from dumping; from installations and devices used in the exploration and exploitation of marine resources, etc.  However, it places the greatest emphasis on vessel source pollution.

States are required under the Convention to adopt in the EEZ laws and regulations for the prevention, reduction and control of pollution from vessels conforming to and giving effect to generally accepted international standards established through the competent international organisation or general diplomatic conference.47 The Convention does not expressly state what this “competent international organisation” is. However, the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) is acknowledged as the organisation concern.48 Therefore the IMO rules; especially the International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships as amended by its protocols (MARPOL 73/78) are to be the basis of enforcement in the EEZ. Indeed, one delegation stated in an informal negotiating group of the Third Committee of UNCLOS that the international rules and standards refer to the existing IMO treaties of 1954, 1973 and to future IMO treaties; this was not contradicted.49 Prior to UNCLOS, states enforcement of pollution standards was generally limited to the territorial seas. On the high seas, the responsibility was that of flag states. The Convention strengthens the flag state regime of enforcement. In addition, it introduces port and coastal state enforcement rights in the EEZ.

Port-State Enforcement

Under article 218 of the Convention a vessel that is voluntarily in the port of a coastal state may be arrested and prosecuted for violations of pollution standards irrespective of where it occurred — be it in the coastal or some other state's EEZ, the high seas or territorial seas.

Port-state enforcement should provide an alternative means of enforcement, especially for developing states that lack the necessary technical and manpower requirements to police the vast expanse of water in the EEZ. This is because many of the vessels that navigate through the EEZ usually call at ports.50 The technical and manpower limitations are not with developing states alone. The cost of regular air and sea patrols is such that many developed states have had to rely almost completely on reports of aircraft and vessels engaged on other duties. Even the United States Coast Guard engages in little surveillance outside the harbours. Similarly, Canadian enforcement in the EEZ is limited to the Department of National Defence (DND) surveillance flights.51

Port-state enforcement underlies the global nature of pollution and the need for international means to combat it.  For without it, even if a state could eliminate pollution within its EEZ by effective standards and enforcement measures, it could not protect itself from discharges occurring just beyond this zone that are carried landwards by winds and currents.52

The port-state enforcement provision is couched in permissive terms and like coastal state enforcement is made subject to the safeguard provisions under the Convention.53 They are also aimed at protecting international navigation from abuse and thus help ensure that careful balance required between coastal states’ rights and navigational interests.

The Convention provides for the observance of the "due process” requirement in proceedings; that enforcement may only be exercised by states' officials, warships, military aircraft, or other ship or aircraft marked and identifiable as being on government service; that enforcement should not cause hazard to vessels or their crew; and that a vessel should be promptly released on the posting of a bond. In addition, states shall not delay a foreign vessel longer than necessary.  Inspections are limited generally to those documents required under the IMO Conventions. Enforcement is to be exercised in a non-discriminatory way and only monetary penalties may be imposed upon foreign vessels. A three-year period of limitation for proceedings as well as a "double jeopardy" clause are also provided for.

The most important and also the most controversial of the safeguard provisions is the flag-state pre-emption clause.54 This provision enables flag-states to suspend proceedings instituted by coastal or port-states within six months of the institution of such proceedings and to undertake its own proceedings. This provision must, however, not be viewed as detracting from the regime. The rationale behind the rule is that before allowing coastal or port-state to take proceedings and impose penalties on foreign vessels, the flag-state should be given reasonable opportunity to exercise its enforcement rights and carry out its obligations which are directly derived from the relation of the ship to this state.55

As formulated, the provision is not an immutable precept that allows flag-states to pre-empt proceedings anyhow. The right of pre-emption does not apply in the case of "major damage." What amounts to a major damage is not defined. Hence a coastal state could deny a flag-state pre-emption rights on the ground that the polluting vessel’s damage amounts to “major damage.” Secondly, it does not apply where a flag-state has repeatedly disregarded its enforcement obligations. A flag-state that “pre-empts” proceedings without imposing any sanctions on its vessels could find itself being denied this right in subsequent proceedings involving its vessels. This is more so since flag-states are obliged to inform coastal or port-states of the outcome of such proceedings. Above all, states parties would have to ensure that their legislation conform with the Convention’s texts.  So long as the rules, especially penalties are uniform, flag-state pre-emption pales into insignificance.56

Coastal State Enforcement

Article 220 provides for state coastal enforcement. It states:

“When a vessel is voluntarily within a port or at an off-shore terminal of a State, that State may, subject to section 7, institute proceedings in respect of any violation of its laws and regulations adopted in accordance with this Convention or applicable international rules and standards or for the prevention, reduction and control of pollution from vessels when the violation has occurred within the exclusive economic zone of that State …”

This provision like that on port-state enforcement is couched in permissive and not mandatory language. Coastal-state enforcement involves surveillance, inspections, investigations and prosecutions. Inspections and investigations are to be carried out as is provided for under port-state enforcement.57 Outside its ports and within the zone, the enforcement powers are circumscribed. This is owing to the sui generis character of the zone which requires that a careful balance be maintained between the interests of coastal states and navigational rights as a whole.

Coastal-state enforcement power in the EEZ is limited to information gathering and inspections, the latter action being under stated conditions. There must be "clear grounds" for believing that the vessel has violated international rules and standards governing vessel-source pollution. Further, the violation must have resulted in "substantial discharge causing or threatening significant pollution of the marine environment." In addition, inspection is justified only when the vessel has refused to give information or “if the information supplies is at variance with the evident factual situation and if the circumstances of the case justify such inspection."58

After inspection, and where violations are established, the coastal state may either await the arrival of the vessel in its ports so as to commence proceedings, or transmit the necessary evidence to the next port of call for necessary action. In this connection, article 230(3) allows the coastal state to require information regarding the identity and port of registry, the last and next port of call, and other relevant information required to establish whether a violation has occurred.

Article 220(4) of the Convention requires port-states to act as far as practicable on this request. Flag states are also to adopt laws and regulations and to take measures to ensure that vessels flying their flags comply with such request.

Admittedly, the success of the system depends on co-operation between states, but in view of the global nature of the pollution problem, there is every incentive for states to collectively act against it; and also in fulfilment of their Convention obligations. In addition, where there is "clear objective evidence" that a violation has resulted in "discharge causing a major damage or threat of major damage" to the EEZ or its resources, the coastal state may institute proceedings including detention of the vessel in accordance with its laws (article 220(6)). In this connection, it may exercise the right of hot pursuit. The Convention neither defines "clear grounds", "objective evidence", nor "substantial discharge." Presumably, it leaves that for the determination of coastal states. In view of the rights that these states have over the resources of the zone, and in spite of the circumscribed nature of coastal state enforcement powers, there is immense potential for coastal states to determine these standards in such a manner as to enable them exercise greater enforcement powers than the Convention's stipulation in the name of resource conservation.

Sovereign Immunity

The Convention's environmental protection and preservation provisions are made subject to the "sovereign immunity" provisions under article 236.59 The issue of immunity was not extensively discussed at UNCLOS; the Convention follows the example of other existing treaties by granting immunity to such vessels. Under article 236, warships, naval auxiliary as well as other vessels or aircraft owned or operated by a state and used on government non-commercial service are exempted from coastal states enforcement jurisdiction. In this wise, the Convention adopts the restrictive immunity doctrine under international law.  It must however be emphasised that the immunity is from arrest and prosecution and not immunity from liability.60 Consequently, such vessels are legally obliged under article 236 to comply with the Convention’s texts.  Provision of immunity under article 236 is thus no licence to pollute.

ÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿÿ the interests of coastal states" or vice versa. The problem posed by article 59 is also accentuated by the fact that certain vital issues; namely fisheries and marine scientific research that are likely to lead to conflicts are exempted from the compulsory dispute settlement procedures and made subject to coastal states' sole discretionary competence. Ghana as a coastal state could, for instance, couch its regulatory interference with navigation on the basis of resource conservation, since marine pollution could be harmful to fisheries. Such a measure, however, is likely to be resisted by maritime states, since navigational freedoms will thereby be infringed. In this lies the potential for more conflicts; the very issue the Convention seeks to avoid.

CONCLUDING NOTE ON THE EEZ

The EEZ as now a firmly established institution of international law. It is neither the high seas, nor the territorial seas; but a zone sui generis of multifunctional competence.  It is a cause championed especially by developing states like Ghana who see resources in the zone as constituting an important basis for socio-economic development. Virtually all these states had been colonies of the Western powers. Independence meant their ability to do away with the conservative territorial sea breadths of their colonial masters in order to increase their wealth from the seas. The pre-UNCLOS regime of territorial/high sea dichotomy was not a suitable medium for the attainment of these objectives. The notion of high seas meant that all states had rights to the resources of the area whilst an expansion of the territorial sea out to 200 miles meant an infringement of navigational rights which the maritime states were not predisposed to accept. The EEZ emerged as a compromise aimed at accommodating the two interests.

 

Admittedly, there is at present lack of uniformity and consistency in state practice on the matter. However, the effect of the Convention would be to engender the required uniformity so as to bring out clearly the nature of the zone: an area where resource rights enure mainly to the coastal state, whilst all other states have the necessary communication and transit rights.

Ghana, the EEZ and the Future

The EEZ concept as a cause championed by Ghana and other developing states is now a well-established institution of international law. The resources of the EEZ, properly explored, exploited and conserved, should be a veritable means for the enhancement of Ghana's socio-economic development. In this regard, a lot needs to be done to realise the full potentials of our EEZ. I propose the following as my contribution to the subject.

(1) There is the need to have the EEZ firmly established. Legislation per se is not sufficient. This must be accompanied by the provision of charts or scales showing the outer limits of the EEZ so that the same can be ascertained.  Due publicity of these charts should be given to the international community and copies of the same should be lodged with the United Nations.68 We need also to delimit our EEZ with our adjacent neighbouring states. Indications are that hydrocarbons do exist in commercial quantities in the western part of our EEZ. There is thus the need to demarcate our borders with our neighbours so as to avoid the potential of a conflict in the future.69

(2) We should create the necessary legal and institutional frameworks for the exploration, exploitation and utilisation of our resources in our EEZ. A beginning has been made by the Fisheries and Petroleum Laws. These, however, have to be modified and/or updated to be in line with the Convention's stipulations which are the norms or benchmark against which the validity or otherwise of such legislations may be assessed. In this regard, our laws on the protection of the marine environment require a drastic overhaul to be in conformity with the IMO and other international convenants on the subject. There is also the requirement for national legislation on the various aspects of the EEZ such as scientific research, navigation and the construction of artificial islands installation and structures.

(3) As a nation, we must be prepared to devote some of our limited resources towards the policing of our EEZ. In this regard, the unit ought to be made functional through the provision of the requisite tools for its functions. This is admittedly an expensive venture. However, the long terms benefits are certainly worthwhile: a check on poaching and pollution in our EEZ would mean conservation of our living resources. Besides, the fines imposed on arrested poachers could be a valuable source of funding for the operations of the unit.

(4) The problems of the seas are closely interrelated and must be considered as a whole. They have international dimensions as well. Pollutants, for example know of no national frontiers so that even when localised, the effects of winds, currents and waves result in a spread of pollutants beyond national frontiers. Similarly, fisheries cannot be confined in a national EEZ. There is thus the need for Ghana to co-operate with our neighbours in the sub-region to tackle the problems of the EEZ, especially the marine pollution problem.  In this regard, the coastal and port-state enforcement mechanisms under the Convention are welcome inroads into the enforcement system.  Fortunately, there is in existence the Action Plan for the Protection and Development of the Marine and Coastal Environment of the West and Central African Region (WACAF), one of UNEP’s regional seas programmes and the 1991 Dakar Convention on Fisheries Co-operation among African States bordering the Atlantic Ocean within whose framework the issues of marine environmental protection and resource conservation can be addressed. Ghana as a member of these institutions should, together with other states parties, intensify their co-operation in these fields within the framework of these organisations.

(5) Finally, as a nation, we must adopt a maritime outlook. So far our policies seem to have focused on exploration, exploitation and conservation of our land-based resources to the relative neglect of our maritime resources.

note 30.

44  Article 73, the relevant provision, provides: “

1  The coastal State may, in the exercise of its sovereign rights to explore, exploit, conserve and manage the living resources in the exclusive economic zone, take such measure, including boarding, inspection, arrest and judicial proceedings, as may be necessary to ensure compliance with the laws and regulations adopted by it in conformity with this Convention.

2   Arrested vessels and their crews shall be promptly released upon the posting of reasonable bond or other security.

3  Coastal State penalties for violations of fisheries laws and regulations in the exclusive economic zone may not include imprisonment, in the absence of agreements to the contrary by the States concerned, or any other form of corporal punishment.

4   In case of arrest or detention of foreign vessels the coastal State shall promptly notify the flag State, through appropriate channels of the action taken and of any penalties subsequently imposed.”

45 Currently the Fisheries Department relies heavily on local fishermen and the Ghana Navy to police the EEZ. Very little is accomplished in the area of enforcement.

59   Article 236 reads:

“The provisions of this Convention regarding the protection and preservation of the marine environment do not apply to any warship, naval auxiliary, other vessels or aircraft owned or operated by a State and used for the time being only on government non-commercial service. However, each State shall ensure, by the adoption of appropriate measures not impairing operations or operational capabilities of such vessels or aircraft owned or operated by it, that such vessels or aircraft owned or operated by it, that such vessels or aircraft act in a manner consistent so far as is reasonable and practicable, with this Convention.”

62  OILPOL 54 completely banned operational discharges of oil from ships except where the total discharge on a ballast voyage did not exceed 60 litres per mile travelled by the ship.  OILPOL 54 also prohibited discharge of oil from cargo spaces of tanks within 50 miles of the nearest land; and introduced a new form of oil record book designed to show the movement of cargo oil and its residue from loading to discharging, on a tank-to-tank basis.  Finally, for other ships, OILPOL 54 placed a limited on rate of discharge at not more than 60 litres per mile travelled by the ship.  With regard to the oil content of any bilge water discharged from ships, it was not to be less than 100 parts per million.  The 1971 amendment to OILPOL 54 provided greater protection to the Greater Barrier Reef of Australia and also limited the size of tanks on oil tankers, thereby minimizing the amount of oil that could escape in the event of an accident.  See L J H Legault, “Freedom of the Seas:  A Licence to Pollute?” (1971) 21 U of T L J 211.

63  (1973) 12 Int’l Leg Mat 1319; (1978) 17 Int’l Leg Mat 579.

64  The oil pollution regulations apply to all tanks and ships of 150 and 400 gross tons and above respectively.  MARPOL 73/78 imposes an absolute prohibition on oil discharge in special areas.  The Convention also requires state parties to provide adequate facilities for the reception of oily mixtures in places like loading terminals and ports. In addition, it provides that oil tankers must be equipped with facilities like slop tanks, oily water separating equipment, or filtering systems for the discharge of machinery, space bilges and oil monitoring devices. The latter helps detect the amount of oil discharged by vessels upon inspection. MARPOL 73/78 also retains the limitations on tank size imposed by the 1971 amendment to OILPOL 54. Furthermore, it requires all new oil tankers of 20,000 tons dead weight and above to be provided with segregated ballast tanks (SBTS) of sufficient capacity to enable them to operate on ballast voyages without using cargo tanks for ballast purposes; and since SBTS are not used for carrying oil, there are no oily mixtures and hence no pollution. Provision is also made for crude oil washing (COW) as an alternative requirement in SBTS on existing tankers, and is an additional requirement for new tankers. Under COW, tanks are washed with crude oil and not with water. The crude oil's solvent action makes the cleaning process far more effective than when water is used. Besides, there is no mixture of the oil and water that caused so much operational pollution in the past. The system, however, poses operational dangers because of the attendant build-up of explosive gases in cargo tanks. It is for this reason that the SOLAS 74 protocol prescribes the use of inert gas systems on such vessels. To ensure that tankers in the event of accidents survive bottom damage, MARPOL 73/78 has introduced new subdivision and stability requirements based on ship lengths. There is the requirement for an initial survey before a ship is put into service or before an IOPCC is issued, as well as periodical surveys of vessels at intervals not exceeding five years. The application of MARPOL 73/78's technical requirements to “new tankers” has meant that most vessels ordered since December 1975 have incorporated the requirements of MARPOL 73/78: (1982) IMO News 4; (1983) IMO News 7.

65 As at 3 December 1991, MARPOL 73/78 had 70 contacting state parties the combined merchant fleet of which constitutes approximately 90 per cent of the gross registered tonnage (grt) of the world's merchant fleet; LL 1966 as amended had 124 states parties with approximately 98 per cent of the world's grt; SOLAS 74 had 116 parties with approximately 97 per cent of the world's grt; COLREG 72 had 113 states parties with approximately 96 per cent of the world's grt and STCW 1978 had 89 states parties with approximately 83 per cent of the world's grt. Ghana, however, is not a party to the Global Convention on the Prevention of Marine Pollution by Dumping of Wastes and other Matter as amended   (LDC 1972) which governs the subject of dumping, currently very topical in the West African sub-region. On Ghana's ratification of these IMO Conventions and their status: see “Status of Multilateral Conventions and Instruments in Respect of which the International Maritime Organisation or its Secretary-General Performs Depository or other Functions” as at 31 December 1991, IMO Doc 1/2735/Rev/6.
